This is the correctness oracle. The agent's solution must produce the same
top-k values (and equivalent indices modulo ties) within the tolerance
declared in problem.yaml. Note that solution.py is FORBIDDEN from calling
torch.topk / torch.sort / torch.kthvalue (see problem.yaml).

HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000", "H100", "B200"]

    """Top-k over the last dim of a 2D tensor.

    Input:
        x: (batch, n) fp32
    Output:
        values:  (batch, k) fp32, sorted descending
        indices: (batch, k) int64, into the last dim of x
    """

    def __init__(self, batch: int, n: int, k: int):
        super().__init__()
        self.batch, self.n, self.k = batch, n, k
        # No learned parameters, but declare a dummy buffer so state_dict
        # is non-empty and load_state_dict(strict=True) is meaningful.
        self.register_buffer("_dummy", torch

     returned sorted descending, so positional comparison is well-defined.

"""Canonical shape sweep for TopK.

Mix of:
  - decoder vocab top-k (single sequence, very large n, moderate k) — pure
    bandwidth test; the input read dominates everything.
  - prefill / batched attention top-k (many rows, moderate n, small k) — tests
    per-row parallelism and shared-memory bitonic networks.
  - non-power-of-2 n stress case — bitonic sort networks naturally want
    powers of two; this forces the agent to handle padding or partial sorts.
  - small-k limit — k=1 (argmax) is a degenerate but useful sanity case.
"""

SHAPES = [
    {"batch": 1,   "n": 131072, "k": 64},   # decoder vocab top-k (Llama vocab ~128k)
    {"batch": 64,  "n": 8192,   "k": 8},    # prefill / attention top-k
    {"batch": 32,  "n": 16384,  "k": 32},   # mid-size batched
    {"batch": 16,  "n": 12000,  "k": 16},   # non-power-of-2 n stress
    {"batch": 128, "n": 4096,   "k": 1},    # batched argmax (k=1 corner case)
]

"""SOTA reference for TopK: torch.topk itself.

does better on the (batch, n, k) shape mix we evaluate — Faiss BlockSelect is
specialized for k>=32 with much larger n, and CUB's DeviceSegmentedRadixSort
sorts the full row (overkill for top-k).

This file is INTENTIONALLY allowed to call torch.topk because it is the SOTA
oracle, not the agent's

Options: 1. **Triton bitonic sort** - clean, but may not beat torch.topk 2. **CUDA C++ with bitonic sort** - more control, can use warp primitives 3. **CUDA with register-based bitonic networks** - very fast for small k 4. **Inline PTX** - ultimate control but harder For large n small k (decoder vocab), the key is minimizing memory traffic. For small n (4096) and k=1, this is argmax - can use warp reductions. For n=8192, k=8 and n=16384, k=32, we need efficient per-row selection. Let me think about heap vs bitonic for top-k: - For small k (<=64), a sorted-register array with insertion (linear shift) or bitonic merge is efficient. - Bitonic network sorts a fixed-size array; we can maintain top-k by merging tiles. A known efficient approach: "warp-select" / Faiss blockSelect style: - Each warp maintains k best values in registers (sorted) - For each new value, compare against worst-of-k and if better, insert - After processing row, write out sorted top-k For k=64, maintaining sorted array in registers is feasible (64 registers per warp, plus indices = 128 registers, manageable). But to saturate memory, we need enough warps reading concurrently. With batch=1, n=131072, one row only - we need intra-row parallelism. Use a block with multiple warps, each processing a chunk, then merge. Actually for single row large n, we can: - Launch many blocks all processing the same row but different tiles - Each block maintains top-k in shared memory - Atomically/global merge? Or do a final reduction. Better: single block with many warps processing the row. But max 32 warps/block. Each warp processes n/32 elements. Then 32 warp-local top-ks merge into block top-k. cat > /tmp/profile_topk.py << 'EOF'
import torch
import time
from shapes import SHAPES

device = torch.device('cuda:0')
torch.manual_seed(42)

for shape in SHAPES:
    b, n, k = shape['batch'], shape['n'], shape['k']
    x = torch.randn(b, n, device=device, dtype=torch.float32)
    
    # warmup
    for _ in range(10):
        v, i = torch.topk(x, k, dim=-1)
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    
    # time
    iters = 100
    start = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    end = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    start.record()
    for _ in range(iters):
        v, i = torch.topk(x, k, dim=-1)
    end.record()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    ms = start.elapsed_time(end) / iters
    
    bytes_moved = b * n * 4 + b * k * (4 + 8)
    gbps = bytes_moved / (ms * 1e6)
    print(f"shape=({b},{n},{k}) ms={ms:.3f} gbps={gbps:.1f}")
EOF

stdout (4313 chars) — TRUNCATED
"""Centralized GPU timing for benchmark.py files.

All problems' benchmark.py should call `time_fn` from here so we have one
implementation of warmup, L2 flush, and CUDA event capture to maintain.

Methodology:
  - 10 warmup calls absorb Triton autotune (typical ~7 configs) and
    torch.compile reduce-overhead CUDA-graph capture.
  - Between each timed call, `_l2_flush()` writes 128 MB to evict L2
    (Blackwell L2 is 96 MB; we want any prior L2 contents flushed so
    we measure HBM-load bandwidth, not L2-cached bandwidth).
  - GPU timing via cuda.Event with synchronize() AFTER record() but
    BEFORE elapsed_time() — the canonical NVIDIA pattern.
  - Reported value is the median of `iters` trials, robust to outliers.

Notes / known biases not addressed here:
  - torch.compile(mode="reduce-overhead") gets CUDA graphs which eliminate
    launch overhead. Custom Triton/CUDA kernels do NOT get this treatment.
    On small shapes where launch overhead matters, this gives compile an
    artificial advantage. We accept this as the cost of using the agreed
    "compiled" baseline policy.
  - cuBLAS / cuDNN allocate workspaces on first call. 10 warmup absorbs.
  - Median over a fairly small number of trials (default 30) is fine for
    headline numbers but won't catch bimodal distributions. Use --extra
    instrumentation if you ever care.
"""
from __future__ import annotations

import os
import re
import statistics
import time
from datetime import UTC, datetime

import torch

# Bl

Actually, CUDA event recorded in the stream includes the kernel execution time. Launch overhead is before s.record()? No - record inserts a marker into the stream. The kernel launch is async, so s.record() may complete before the kernel starts. The elapsed time measured is GPU time between the events reaching the GPU, which includes kernel execution but not much CPU launch overhead. So the measured times are GPU execution times. Pseudocode: ```cuda template<int BLOCK, int TILE, int K> __global__ void topk_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ out_val, int64_t* __restrict__ out_idx, int B, int N, int K_actual) { int row = blockIdx.x; if (row >= B) return; const float* row_x = x + row * N; float* row_out = out_val + row * K_actual; int64_t* row_idx = out_idx + row * K_actual; extern __shared__ char smem[]; // Layout: [running_val K][running_idx K][tile_val TILE][tile_idx TILE] float* run_val = (float*)smem; int* run_idx = (int*)(run_val + K); float* tile_val = (float*)(run_idx + K); int* tile_idx = (int*)(tile_val + TILE); // Initialize running top-k with -inf, idx=0 for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < K; i += BLOCK) { run_val[i] = -CUDART_INF_F; run_idx[i] = 0; } __syncthreads(); int num_tiles = (N + TILE - 1) / TILE; for (int t = 0; t < num_tiles; ++t) { int tile_start = t * TILE; int tile_end = min(tile_start + TILE, N); int tile_len = tile_end - tile_start; // Load tile for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < TILE; i += BLOCK) { if (i < tile_len) { tile_val[i] = row_x[tile_start + i]; tile_idx[i] = tile_start + i; } else { tile_val[i] = -CUDART_INF_F; tile_idx[i] = 0; } } __syncthreads(); // Bitonic sort tile descending bitonic_sort_desc<TILE>(tile_val, tile_idx); // Copy top K of tile to upper half of merge buffer // We'll sort 2K elements: run_val[0:K] + tile_val[0:K] // Place tile top-K after run top-K for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < K; i += BLOCK) { tile_val[i + K] = tile_val[i]; // wait, need scratch tile_idx[i + K] = tile_idx[i]; } __syncthreads(); // Better: merge buffer of size 2K: run_val[0:K] then tile_val[0:K] // But tile_val is TILE sized. We can reuse tile_val[0:2K] as merge buffer. // Place tile top-K at tile_val[K:2K] // Then sort tile_val[0:2K] // Then copy back to run_val[0:K] for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < K; i += BLOCK) { tile_val[K + i] = tile_val[i]; tile_idx[K + i] = tile_idx[i]; } __syncthreads(); // Now tile_val[0:K] still has old tile sorted top-K, tile_val[K:2K] has copy // We need run_val[0:K] + tile_val[0:K] in a contiguous buffer. // Better layout: merge_val[2K], merge_idx[2K] // Put run in [0:K], tile in [K:2K], sort, copy first K to run. } } ``` This is getting messy with shared memory layout. Let me design carefully. Simpler shared memory layout: - `float s_val[TILE + 2*K];` (actually need TILE for tile, plus 2K for merge) - `int s_idx[TILE + 2*K];` - Running top-K stored at `s_val[0:K]` - Merge buffer at `s_val[K:3K]`? No. Alternative: dedicated arrays: - `float run_val[K], run_idx[K]` - `float merge_val[2K], merge_idx[2K]` - `float tile_val[TILE], tile_idx[TILE]` Total: (K + 2K + TILE) * (4+4) = (TILE + 3K) * 8 bytes For TILE=1024, K=64: (1024+192)*8 = 9728 bytes. Tiny. OK let me code this. For bitonic sort, I'll write a template function that sorts a shared memory array of size N (power of 2) descending. ```cuda template<int N> __device__ void bitonic_sort_desc(float* val, int* idx) { for (int size = 2; size <= N; size *= 2) { for (int stride = size / 2; stride > 0; stride /= 2) { int tid = threadIdx.x; // Each thread handles multiple elements for (int i = tid; i < N / 2; i += blockDim.x) { int j = i ^ stride; // partner? Need correct bitonic partner formula // Actually bitonic partner depends on stage ... } __syncthreads(); } } } ``` Wait, the bitonic sort partner formula is: - For each element i, partner is i ^ stride, but only within the current subsequence of length `size`. - The direction (asc/desc) alternates or depends on which subsequence. Standard bitonic sort for descending: ```cuda for (k = 2; k <= N; k *= 2) { for (j = k/2; j > 0; j /= 2) { for i in 0..N-1: int ij = i ^ j; if (ij > i) { // process each pair once bool dir = ((i & k) == 0); // direction: for first half of size-k block, sort asc or desc // For full descending sort: dir = ((i / k) % 2 == 0) ? desc : asc? // Actually standard: in stage k, blocks of size k are sorted in alternating directions, // then merged to produce sorted blocks of size 2k. } } } ``` Actually for sorting the entire array in descending order: - Stage size k goes 2, 4, 8, ..., N - Within each stage, we have bitonic sequences of length k, and we sort/merge them to get sequences of length 2k (or for the final stage, length N). - Direction of each length-k block alternates, but after merging they form a bitonic sequence for next stage. Standard implementation for ascending: ```cpp for (int k = 2; k <= n; k *= 2) { for (int j = k/2; j > 0; j /= 2) { for (int i = 0; i < n; ++i) { int l = i ^ j; if (l > i) { bool dir = ((i & k) == 0); if (dir == (a[i] > a[l])) swap(a[i], a[l]); } } } } ``` For descending, change comparison: `dir == (a[i] < a[l])` or similar. Let me derive. In bitonic sort, for a bitonic sequence, we compare-swap pairs (i, i^j). The direction bit determines whether we want min-up or max-up. For descending final sort, we want larger elements at lower indices. A common descending sort: ```cpp bool ascending = false; // for descending for (k=2; k<=N; k*=2) { for (j=k/2; j>0; j/=2) { bool dir = ((i&k)==0) ^ ascending; // or something if (dir && a[i] < a[j]) swap; // descending if (!dir && a[i] > a[j]) swap; // ascending } } ``` Let me just test with a known implementation. I'll write a small CPU test. Actually, there's an even simpler approach: use `std::sort` or `thrust::sort` on small arrays in shared memory? No, we need parallelism. OK let me write the bitonic sort carefully. I'll use the formulation: For element i and partner l = i ^ j (where j is stride), we swap if they are in the wrong order. The order direction for pair (i,l) is determined by the bit at position log2(k) of i (or l). If `(i & k) == 0`, the pair should be in descending order (larger first, i < l means a[i] >= a[l]). If `(i & k) != 0`, the pair should be in ascending order. Wait that's for the merge step within bitonic sort where each block of size k is sorted ascending then descending to form a bitonic sequence, then merged. Let me just use a tested snippet. cat > /tmp/test_bitonic.py << 'EOF'
import numpy as np

def bitonic_sort_desc(a):
    n = len(a)
    assert (n & (n-1)) == 0
    a = a.copy()
    k = 2
    while k <= n:
        j = k // 2
        while j > 0:
            for i in range(n):
                l = i ^ j
                if l > i:
                    # direction: if (i & k) == 0, we want descending (a[i] >= a[l])
                    # else ascending (a[i] <= a[l])
                    if (i & k) == 0:
                        if a[i] < a[l]:
                            a[i], a[l] = a[l], a[i]
                    else:
                        if a[i] > a[l]:
                            a[i], a[l] = a[l], a[i]
            j //= 2
        k *= 2
    return a

for n in [2,4,8,16,32,64,128]:
    a = np.random.randn(n)
    ref = np.sort(a)[::-1]
    got = bitonic_sort_desc(a)
    assert np.allclose(got, ref), f"fail n={n}"
print("bitonic descending sort OK")
EOF

In the inner loop: - For stride j, pairs are (i, i^j) for i from 0 to N-1, but only process each pair once (when l > i). - Each thread can process multiple i's with step BLOCK. - After each stride, __syncthreads(). +"""Custom CUDA top-k kernel using shared-memory bitonic sort on tiles.
+
+One block per row; each tile is fully sorted descending and merged with a
+running top-k buffer.  k=1 is handled with a dedicated warp-reduction argmax.
+"""
+import os
+import torch
+import torch.nn as nn
+from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline
+
+
+_CUDA_SOURCE = r"""
+#include <cuda_runtime.h>
+#include <cfloat.h>
+#include <stdint.h>
+
+// ----------------------------------------------------------------------------
+// Bitonic sort helpers (descending by value; indices are carried along)
+// ----------------------------------------------------------------------------
+
+__device__ __forceinline__ void swap(float& v1, int& i1, float& v2, int& i2) {
+    if (v1 < v2) {
+        float tv = v1; v1 = v2; v2 = tv;
+        int ti = i1; i1 = i2; i2 = ti;
+    }
+}
+
+// Sort the first N (power-of-two) entries of val/idx in shared memory,
+// descending by value.
+template <int N>
+__device__ void bitonic_sort_desc(float* __restrict__ val,
+                                  int*   __restrict__ idx) {
+    #pragma unroll
+    for (int k = 2; k <= N; k <<= 1) {
+        #pragma unroll
+        for (int j = k >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) {
+            int tid = threadIdx.x;
+            for (int i = tid; i < N / 2; i += blockDim.x) {
+                int lo = i;
+                int hi = i ^ j;
+                if (hi > lo) {
+                    if ((lo & k) == 0) {
+                        // want descending: larger at lower index
+                        if (val[lo] < val[hi]) {
+                            float tv = val[lo]; val[lo] = val[hi]; val[hi] = tv;
+                            int ti = idx[lo]; idx[lo] = idx[hi]; idx[hi] = ti;
+                        }
+                    } else {
+                        // want ascending: larger at higher index
+                        if (val[lo] > val[hi]) {
+                            float tv = val[lo]; val[lo] = val[hi]; val[hi] = tv;
+                            int ti = idx[lo]; idx[lo] = idx[hi]; idx[hi] = ti;
+                        }
+                    }
+                }
+            }
+            __syncthreads();
+        }
+    }
+}
+
+// ----------------------------------------------------------------------------
+// k = 1  (argmax)
+// ----------------------------------------------------------------------------
+__global__ void topk_k1_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x,
+                               float* __restrict__ out_val,
+                               int64_t* __restrict__ out_idx,
+                               int B, int N) {
+    int row = blockIdx.x;
+    if (row >= B) return;
+    const float* row_x = x + (int64_t)row * N;
+
+    float best_val = -FLT_MAX;
+    int best_idx = 0;
+
+    int tid = threadIdx.x;
+    int nt = blockDim.x;
+    for (int i = tid; i < N; i += nt) {
+        float v = row_x[i];
+        if (v > best_val) {
+            best_val = v;
+            best_idx = i;
+        }
+    }
+
+    // warp reduce
+    #pragma unroll
+    for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) {
+        float other_v = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, best_val, offset);
+        int other_i = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, best_idx, offset);
+        if (other_v > best_val) {
+            best_val = other_v;
+            best_idx = other_i;
+        }
+    }
+
+    if (tid == 0) {
+        out_val[row] = best_val;
+        out_idx[row] = (int64_t)best_idx;
+    }
+}
+
+// ----------------------------------------------------------------------------
+// General k > 1
+// ----------------------------------------------------------------------------
+template <int BLOCK, int TILE, int K>
+__global__ void topk_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x,
+                            float* __restrict__ out_val,
+                            int64_t* __restrict__ out_idx,
+                            int B, int N) {
+    int row = blockIdx.x;
+    if (row >= B) return;
+    const float* row_x = x + (int64_t)row * N;
+    float* row_out_v = out_val + (int64_t)row * K;
+    int64_t* row_out_i = out_idx + (int64_t)row * K;
+
+    extern __shared__ char smem[];
+    float* run_val = (float*)smem;
+    int*   run_idx = (int*)(run_val + K);
+    float* tile_val = (float*)(run_idx + K);
+    int*   tile_idx = (int*)(tile_val + TILE);
+    float* merge_val = (float*)(tile_idx + TILE);
+    int*   merge_idx = (int*)(merge_val + 2 * K);
+
+    // Initialize running top-k with -inf
+    for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < K; i += BLOCK) {
+        run_val[i] = -FLT_MAX;
+        run_idx[i] = 0;
+    }
+    __syncthreads();
+
+    int tiles = (N + TILE - 1) / TILE;
+    for (int t = 0; t < tiles; ++t) {
+        int base = t * TILE;
+        int len = (base + TILE <= N) ? TILE : (N - base);
+
+        // Load tile, padding with -inf
+        for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < TILE; i += BLOCK) {
+            if (i < len) {
+                tile_val[i] = row_x[base + i];
+                tile_idx[i] = base + i;
+            } else {
+                tile_val[i] = -FLT_MAX;
+                tile_idx[i] = 0;
+            }
+        }
+        __syncthreads();
+
+        // Sort tile descending
+        bitonic_sort_desc<TILE>(tile_val, tile_idx);
+
+        // Build merge buffer: running top-k + tile top-k
+        for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < K; i += BLOCK) {
+            merge_val[i] = run_val[i];
+            merge_idx[i] = run_idx[i];
+            merge_val[K + i] = tile_val[i];
+            merge_idx[K + i] = tile_idx[i];
+        }
+        __syncthreads();
+
+        // Sort 2K elements and keep first K
+        bitonic_sort_desc<2 * K>(merge_val, merge_idx);
+
+        for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < K; i += BLOCK) {
+            run_val[i] = merge_val[i];
+            run_idx[i] = merge_idx[i];
+        }
+        __syncthreads();
+    }
+
+    // Write output
+    for (int i = thr

The partner should be within the same block of size k. The formula for partner is: - For each block [m*k, (m+1)*k), the first half and second half form a bitonic sequence. - For stride j, pairs are (i, i+j) for i in first half of block and (i, i-j) for i in second half. The XOR formula `i ^ j` works if we iterate i over the entire range [0, N) and process pairs where the partner is `i ^ j`. But then we process each pair twice (once from each side) or need to be careful. Actually, the standard GPU bitonic sort often uses: ```cuda for (k=2; k<=N; k*=2) { for (j=k/2; j>0; j/=2) { int ixj = threadIdx.x ^ j; if (ixj > threadIdx.x) { // compare a[threadIdx.x] and a[ixj] } } } ``` But this assumes each thread handles one element and N = blockDim.x. The XOR partner is within the same block of size k because k is a power of 2 and j < k, so `i ^ j` differs from i only in bits < log2(k), keeping them in the same k-block. Yes! import numpy as np

def bitonic_sort_desc(a):
    n = len(a)
    assert (n & (n-1)) == 0
    a = a.copy()
    k = 2
    while k <= n:
        j = k // 2
        while j > 0:
            for i in range(n):
                l = i ^ j
                if l > i:
                    if (i & k) == 0:
                        if a[i] < a[l]:
                            a[i], a[l] = a[l], a[i]
                    else:
                        if a[i] > a[l]:
                            a[i], a[l] = a[l], a[i]
            j //= 2
        k *= 2
    return a

n = 1024
a = np.random.randn(n)
ref = np.sort(a)[::-1]
got = bitonic_sort_desc(a)
print('match:', np.allclose(got, ref))
" 2>&1 | tail -5

import torch
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

src = r'''
#include <cuda_runtime.h>
#include <float.h>
#include <stdint.h>
#include <torch/extension.h>
#include <tuple>

template <int N>
__device__ void bitonic_sort_desc(float* __restrict__ val,
                                  int*   __restrict__ idx) {
    #pragma unroll
    for (int k = 2; k <= N; k <<= 1) {
        #pragma unroll
        for (int j = k >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) {
            int tid = threadIdx.x;
            for (int i = tid; i < N / 2; i += blockDim.x) {
                int lo = i;
                int hi = i ^ j;
                if (hi > lo) {
                    if ((lo & k) == 0) {
                        if (val[lo] < val[hi]) {
                            float tv = val[lo]; val[lo] = val[hi]; val[hi] = tv;
                            int ti = idx[lo]; idx[lo] = idx[hi]; idx[hi] = ti;
                        }
                    } else {
                        if (val[lo] > val[hi]) {
                            float tv = val[lo]; val[lo] = val[hi]; val[hi] = tv;
                            int ti = idx[lo]; idx[lo] = idx[hi]; idx[hi] = ti;
                        }
                    }
                }
            }
            __syncthreads();
        }
    }
}

__global__ void sort_kernel(const float* in, float* out, int N) {
    extern __shared__ char smem[];
    float* s = (float*)smem;
    int* idx = (int*)(s + N);
    for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < N; i += blockDim.x) {
        s[i] = in[i];
        idx[i] = i;
    }
    __syncthreads();
    bitonic_sort_desc<1024>(s, idx);
    for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < N; i += blockDim.x) {
        out[i] = s[i];
    }
}

torch::Tensor test_sort(torch::Tensor x) {
    auto out = torch::empty_like(x);
    sort_kernel<<<1, 1024, 1024 * (sizeof(float)+sizeof(int))>>>(
        x.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), 1024);
    return out;
}
'''
cpp = r'''
#include <torch/extension.h>
torch::Tensor test_sort(torch::Tensor x);
'''
mod = load_inline(name='debug_sort', cpp_sources=cpp, cuda_sources=src,
                  functions=['test_sort'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'], verbose=False)

x = torch.randn(1024).cuda()
out = mod.test_sort(x)
ref, _ = torch.sort(x, descending=True)
print('match:', torch.allclose(out, ref, atol=1e-5, rtol=1e-5))
print('max diff:', (out - ref).abs().max().item())
print('out first 10:', out[:10])
print('ref first 10:', ref[:10])

import torch
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

src = r'''
#include <cuda_runtime.h>
#include <float.h>
#include <stdint.h>
#include <torch/extension.h>
#include <tuple>

template <int N>
__device__ void bitonic_sort_desc(float* __restrict__ val,
                                  int*   __restrict__ idx) {
    for (int k = 2; k <= N; k <<= 1) {
        for (int j = k >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) {
            int tid = threadIdx.x;
            for (int i = tid; i < N / 2; i += blockDim.x) {
                int lo = i;
                int hi = i ^ j;
                if (hi > lo) {
                    if ((lo & k) == 0) {
                        if (val[lo] < val[hi]) {
                            float tv = val[lo]; val[lo] = val[hi]; val[hi] = tv;
                            int ti = idx[lo]; idx[lo] = idx[hi]; idx[hi] = ti;
                        }
                    } else {
                        if (val[lo] > val[hi]) {
                            float tv = val[lo]; val[lo] = val[hi]; val[hi] = tv;
                            int ti = idx[lo]; idx[lo] = idx[hi]; idx[hi] = ti;
                        }
                    }
                }
            }
            __syncthreads();
        }
    }
}

__global__ void sort_kernel(const float* in, float* out, int N) {
    extern __shared__ char smem[];
    float* s = (float*)smem;
    int* idx = (int*)(s + N);
    for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < N; i += blockDim.x) {
        s[i] = in[i];
        idx[i] = i;
    }
    __syncthreads();
    bitonic_sort_desc<1024>(s, idx);
    for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < N; i += blockDim.x) {
        out[i] = s[i];
    }
}

torch::Tensor test_sort(torch::Tensor x) {
    auto out = torch::empty_like(x);
    sort_kernel<<<1, 1024, 1024 * (sizeof(float)+sizeof(int))>>>(
        x.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), 1024);
    return out;
}
'''

#include <torch/extension.h>
torch::Tensor test_sort(torch::Tensor x);

cat > /tmp/debug_sort3.py << 'PYEOF'
import torch
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

src = r'''
#include <cuda_runtime.h>
#include <torch/extension.h>

template <int N>
__device__ void bitonic_sort_desc(float* __restrict__ val,
                                  int*   __restrict__ idx) {
    for (int k = 2; k <= N; k <<= 1) {
        for (int j = k >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) {
            int tid = threadIdx.x;
            for (int i = tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x) {
                int lo = i;
                int hi = i ^ j;
                if (hi > lo) {
                    if ((lo & k) == 0) {
                        if (val[lo] < val[hi]) {
                            float tv = val[lo]; val[lo] = val[hi]; val[hi] = tv;
                            int ti = idx[lo]; idx[lo] = idx[hi]; idx[hi] = ti;
                        }
                    } else {
                        if (val[lo] > val[hi]) {
                            float tv = val[lo]; val[lo] = val[hi]; val[hi] = tv;
                            int ti = idx[lo]; idx[lo] = idx[hi]; idx[hi] = ti;
                        }
                    }
                }
            }
            __syncthreads();
        }
    }
}

__global__ void sort_kernel(const float* in, float* out, int N) {
    extern __shared__ char smem[];
    float* s = (float*)smem;
    int* idx = (int*)(s + N);
    for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < N; i += blockDim.x) {
        s[i] = in[i];
        idx[i] = i;
    }
    __syncthreads();
    bitonic_sort_desc<1024>(s, idx);
    for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < N; i += blockDim.x) {
        out[i] = s[i];
    }
}

torch::Tensor test_sort(torch::Tensor x) {
    auto out = torch::empty_like(x);
    sort_kernel<<<1, 1024, 1024 * (sizeof(float)+sizeof(int))>>>(
        x.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), 1024);
    return out;
}
'''
cpp = r'''
#include <torch/extension.h>
torch::Tensor test_sort(torch::Tensor x);
'''
mod = load_inline(name='debug_sort3', cpp_sources=cpp, cuda_sources=src,
                  functions=['test_sort'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'], verbose=False)

x = torch.randn(1024).cuda()
out = mod.test_sort(x)
ref, _ = torch.sort(x, descending=True)
print('match i<N:', torch.allclose(out, ref, atol=1e-5, rtol=1e-5))
print('max diff:', (out - ref).abs().max().item())

My initial kernel is much SLOWER than torch.topk! Shape 0 takes 2.5ms vs torch.topk 0.05ms. That's 50x slower. The bitonic sort with full tile sorting is way too expensive. I need a completely different approach. Full bitonic sort of each tile is O(TILE log^2 TILE) which is too much. Need to switch to a register-based or warp-based top-k that only keeps top-k. Key insight: we only need top-k, not sorted tile. We should maintain a running top-k and only compare each element against the k-th best. For k=64, we can maintain top-64 in registers/shared memory efficiently. Let me redesign the kernel. **New approach: per-warp top-k heap in registers, then merge.** For each row: - Launch one block with W warps (e.g., 16 or 32 warps depending on n) - Each warp processes a chunk of n/W elements - Each warp maintains top-k values in registers (distributed across threads) - After processing, warps merge their top-k via shared memory bitonic sort - Write top-k How to maintain per-warp top-k? For k=64, with 32 threads per warp, each thread maintains 2 values (64 total). We can keep them sorted or unsorted. Option A: unsorted, find max via warp reduction each time. But we need to know the threshold (k-th best). If unsorted, threshold is min of the 64, which requires warp reduce. Option B: sorted. Each thread holds 2 values in sorted order within its local pair, and the warp holds 64 values in sorted order via inter-thread ordering. Hard to maintain. Option C: use a bitonic merge network to merge new values into the sorted top-k. Actually, a simpler robust approach for per-warp top-k: Each thread holds a local buffer of M values (e.g., M = k/32 = 2 for k=64). Unsorted. Periodically (every P elements), we do a warp-level merge to keep top-k. But how to "keep top-k" from 32 threads each with M values + possibly new candidates? Use warp-level bitonic sort on 64 elements (k). Each thread contributes 2 values. Sort descending, then each thread keeps 2. Repeat. Specifically, each thread reads 2 new values at a time (or more), merges with its current 2, keeps top 2 locally? No, that doesn't propagate. Better: each thread reads one value at a time. We want to insert it into the warp's top-64. With sorted top-64 distributed 2 per thread, we can compare against the thread's local threshold and propagate. This is complex. Let me think of a simpler per-block approach using shared memory. **Shared-memory heap approach:** Each block maintains a sorted top-k in shared memory. Each thread reads elements and compares against the k-th element (smallest in top-k). If a value is larger, it needs to be inserted. But inserting into a sorted array in shared memory requires shifting, which can't be parallelized easily without atomics. Alternative: use a small fixed-size buffer and batch inserts. Each thread collects local candidates larger than current threshold. Periodically, the block sorts the union of running top-k and thread candidates, keeps top-k. But this needs synchronization. Actually, here's a much simpler and likely fast approach: **Two-pass block-select:** Pass 1: Each block (or warp) processes the row and produces top-k candidates using a simple per-thread local top-m. Then write all candidates to global memory. Pass 2: A single block sorts all candidates and produces final top-k. But Pass 1 needs to produce enough good candidates. If each thread keeps top-2, and we have 1024 threads, we have 2048 candidates. The true top-64 is definitely among them. Then Pass 2 sorts 2048 to get top-64. This is efficient because Pass 1 is just a linear scan with tiny local buffers (top-2 per thread), and Pass 2 sorts a small fixed array (2048). For n=131072, block=1024 threads, each thread processes 128 elements and keeps top-2. Then we have 2048 candidates. Sort 2048, keep 64. But top-2 per thread may not be enough? Actually yes, the global top-64 must include at least one value from each thread? No. But the union of all thread top-2 has size 2048, and the global top-64 is certainly a subset of the top-2 from each thread? Not necessarily! A thread might have the 3rd largest value in the row but only keep top-2. Then that value would be missed. Wait, if each thread keeps its top-2, and a value is the 3rd largest in a thread's chunk, but globally still in top-64, it could be missed if there are many threads with 2 values larger than it. For 1024 threads, the worst case: each thread has 2 values larger than the global 64th. Then global 64th is not in any thread's top-2. So we'd miss it. So top-2 per thread is not sufficient. Need each thread to keep enough so that the union contains top-k. With t threads, each thread needs to keep at least k candidates? No, if each thread keeps top-k, union has top-k trivially. But that's too much. Actually, if we want the union to contain top-k for any input, each thread must keep top-k. Because a single thread could contain all top-k values if its chunk is unlucky. So per-thread top-k is required in worst case. But for random data, top-m with m << k might suffice. But correctness needs worst case. So per-thread/warp top-k is needed. The question is how to maintain it efficiently. OK let me go back to per-warp top-k. With k=64 and 32-thread warps, each thread can maintain top-2 and periodically merge. But as noted, per-thread top-2 is not sufficient. Wait, but if each WARP maintains top-64 (not each thread), then the union of warp top-64s contains global top-64. With 32 warps, we have 32*64=2048 candidates, then sort. So the question is: how does each warp maintain top-64 efficiently? In a warp, 32 threads. Each thread maintains top-2? After every 64 elements (2 per thread), merge to keep top-64. After merge, each thread again has top-2. So per-warp top-64 is maintained. This works because after merge, the warp collectively holds top-64. Then as more elements come in, each thread holds top-2 plus new values, merge again. But maintaining sorted top-64 across warp requires a bitonic merge. For 64 elements with 32 threads (2 per thread), bitonic sort/merge is manageable. Actually, we can use a simpler approach: each warp maintains an UNSORTED buffer of 64 values (2 per thread). For each new value, compare against the minimum of the 64. To find the minimum, do a warp reduce. If new value > min, replace min with new value. This maintains the set of 64 largest values (but unsorted). At the end, sort the 64 to output. Finding min of 64 values distributed 2 per thread: each thread finds min of its 2, then warp reduce min across 32 threads. ~log(32) = 5 shuffle steps. Then thread holding the min replaces it with new value. This is O(1) per element with shuffles! This is very promising. For k=64, each new element costs: - Load value - Each thread finds local min of its 2 values - Warp reduce min (5 shuffles) - Thread with min compares and conditionally replaces - Possibly need to update index too Wait, but after replacing the min, the buffer is still unsorted. The next element's comparison uses the new min. This works because we're maintaining the k largest values seen so far. The "minimum of top-k" is the threshold; any new value larger than threshold displaces the threshold. This is like a register heap of size k. For k=64, distributed 2 per thread. For k=32, 1 per thread. For k=16, 1 per 2 threads (or each thread holds 1 and we reduce over 16 active threads). For k=8, similar. For k=1, special. For k not dividing 32 evenly, we can round up. Actually, we can always keep k values distributed as ceil(k/32) per thread. Then the threshold is min of all kept values. For k=64, 2 per thread = 64 values. For k=32, 1 per thread = 32 values. For k=16, 1 per thread = 32 values (more than needed). For k=8, 1 per thread = 32 values. This is fine! At the end, sort the kept values and keep top k. But for k=64, 2 per thread, finding min of 64: each thread min of 2, then warp reduce min. For k=32, 1 per thread, warp reduce min directly. For k=16/k=8, 1 per thread but we might want to keep only k. Actually keeping 32 is fine, just sort and take top k. But there's a subtlety: if we keep MORE than k values (e.g., 32 for k=16), the threshold is the min of 32, which is lower than the true k-th threshold. This means we might include values that aren't in top-k. But at the end we sort and keep top k, so we still get the correct top-k. The only downside is that we keep more values, slightly more register/compute, but correctness is fine. Wait, no. If we keep 32 values for k=16, the threshold is the 32nd largest seen so far. A new value only enters if > 32nd largest. But we want to keep top 16. If we keep top 32, we might discard a value that's in top 16? No, because if a value is in top 16, it's certainly in top 32, so it's kept. So keeping more is safe. At the end, sort and take top 16. Correct. Great. So unified approach: each thread keeps V values, where V = ceil(k/32) but at least 1. Total kept per warp = 32*V. We maintain top-(32*V) via min-replacement. Actually, to minimize register usage, we can set V = (k + 31) / 32. For k=64, V=2. For k<=32, V=1. But for k=1, V=1 and warp keeps top-32, then we need top-1. That's fine but more work than dedicated argmax. We can special-case k=1. **Algorithm per warp:** ``` Initialize: each thread has V values = -inf and indices = 0 For each element in warp's chunk: v = load idx = global index // find min of current kept values local_min = min of thread's V values warp_min = warpReduceMin(local_min) if (v > warp_min): // find which thread has warp_min // Replace that thread's value with v // Need to handle multiple values equal to min? ``` Finding which thread has the min: use `__match_any_sync` or ballot? Simpler: after finding warp_min, each thread checks if any of its values equals warp_min (within tolerance? exact equality). If so, replace one such value. But if multiple values equal min, we might replace the wrong one. Since values are floats, exact equality is rare. But if v == warp_min, we could replace or not; either is fine for correctness. Actually, to be safe: after computing warp_min, each thread finds the first of its V values that equals warp_min and marks it for replacement. But multiple threads might think they have the min. We only want one replacement. Use `__ffs(__ballot_sync(...))` to select one thread. Hmm, this adds complexity. Simpler: each thread compares its local min and if v > local_min, replaces its local min. But then more than one replacement could happen per new value, violating top-k invariant. Alternative: use a deterministic selection. Compute `warp_min`. Then use ballot to find the lowest thread whose local_min == warp_min. That thread replaces one of its min values. Actually, we can avoid finding which thread by using a different update rule: each thread, if v > local_min, replaces its local min. This might keep slightly more than intended but... wait no, if all 32 threads replace, we've added 32 new values but only intended to add 1. That breaks the top-k count. Also `__ballot_sync` and `__ffs` are standard. For k=64 (V=2), each thread has 2 values. `local_min_idx` is 0 or 1. Find min of 2. For k<=32 (V=1), local_min_idx = 0. After processing all elements, we have 32*V values in registers per warp. We need to collect them and sort, then write top k. **Cross-warp merge:** After each warp has its top-(32*V), we need to merge across warps in the block to get block top-k. With W warps per block, we have W * 32 * V values. For W=32, V=2: 2048 values. Sort 2048 in shared memory, keep top k. For smaller n, we can use fewer warps. Each warp gets n/32 elements. After warp-local top-(32*V), write all W*32*V values to shared memory. Sort in shared memory using bitonic sort (size = next power of 2 >= W*32*V). Keep top k. For k=64, 2048. Actually, for k=16, V could be 1 (ceil(16/32)=1). We keep 32 per warp, total 1024. Sort 1024, keep top 16. Fine. For k=8, similar. **Summary of new kernel:** 1. One block per row, 1024 threads (32 warps). 2. Each warp processes n/32 elements (with handling for n not divisible by 32). 3. Each thread maintains V = max(1, (k+31)/32) values and indices in registers. 4. For each new element: - Find local min value and its index - Warp reduce to find global min - Ballot to find lane with global min - If new value > global min, that lane replaces its min 5. After all elements, each warp writes its 32*V values and indices to shared memory. 6. Bitonic sort the shared memory array descending. 7. Write top k values and indices to output. This avoids full tile sorts. Each element requires only O(log 32) shuffles for min reduction and O(1) ballot. Very fast. Let me also think about coalescing. +// General k > 1 : warp-local heap + shared-memory merge

+template <int K, int V_PER_THREAD>
+__device__ __forceinline__ void warp_topk_update(float vals[V_PER_THREAD],
+                                                  int   idxs[V_PER_THREAD],
+                                                  float new_val, int new_idx) {
+    // Local minimum within this thread's registers.
+    int local_min_pos = 0;
+    float local_min = vals[0];
+    #pragma unroll
+    for (int p = 1; p < V_PER_THREAD; ++p) {
+        if (vals[p] < local_min) {
+            local_min = vals[p];
+            local_min_pos = p;
+        }
+    }
+
+    // Warp-wide minimum lane.
+    float warp_min = __reduce_min_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_min);
+
+    if (new_val > warp_min) {
+        unsigned ballot = __ballot_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_min == warp_min);
+        int min_lane = __ffs(ballot) - 1;
+        int lane = threadIdx.x & 31;
+        if (lane == min_lane) {
+            vals[local_min_pos] = new_val;
+            idxs[local_min_pos] = new_idx;
+        }
+    }
+}

 // ----------------------------------------------------------------------------
 // General k > 1 : warp-local heap + shared-memory merge
 // ----------------------------------------------------------------------------
+__device__ __forceinline__ float warp_reduce_min(float v) {
+    #pragma unroll
+    for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) {
+        float other = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, v, offset);
+        if (other < v) v = other;
+    }
+    return v;
+}
+
+__device__ __forceinline__ int warp_reduce_argmin(float v, int pos) {
+    #pragma unroll
+    for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) {
+        float other_v = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, v, offset);
+        int other_p = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, pos, offset);
+        if (other_v < v) {
+            v = other_v;
+            pos = other_p;
+        }
+    }
+    return pos;
+}
+

                                                   int   idxs[V_PER_THREAD],

reasoning (433 chars)
The issue is that `heap_size` is a runtime variable, not a compile-time constant, so it can't be used as template argument. `heap_size = HEAP_PER_WARP * WARPS_PER_BLOCK` where both are const ints, but `HEAP_PER_WARP` is a template parameter, so `heap_size` should be compile-time constant... but I declared it as `int heap_size = ...` which makes it runtime. Need to make `heap_size` a `static const int` or `constexpr`. Let me fix.

Actually, here's a cleaner idea: use a **warp-level bitonic sort** on the union of kept and new values, but design it to handle N=64 for k<=32 and N=128 for k=64. For k<=32: - V=1 kept per thread. - Load 1 new per thread. - Each thread now has 2 values: kept and new. - Sort 64 values (2 per thread) descending. - Each thread keeps the larger one (top 32). For k=64: - V=2 kept per thread. - Load 1 new per thread. - Each thread now has 3 values. - Pad to 4 values with -inf. - Sort 128 values (4 per thread) descending. - Each thread keeps top 2. I can write a generic warp-level bitonic sort template for N elements with VPT = N/32 values per thread, consecutive layout. Let me implement `warp_bitonic_sort_desc<N>(float val[N/32], int idx[N/32])`. For each sub-stage (k, j): - For each local value at position `pos` (0..VPT-1), logical index `i = lane * VPT + pos`. - Partner `l = i ^ j`. - If partner is in same thread, compare-swap locally. - Else, determine partner thread and partner local position, shuffle value and index, compare-swap, and write back. To avoid doing VPT separate loops per sub-stage, I can unroll over pos. Same-thread condition: `l / VPT == lane` (integer division), i.e., `(i ^ j) / VPT == i / VPT`. Since `i = lane*VPT + pos`, `i/VPT = lane`. So same thread if `((lane*VPT + pos) ^ j) / VPT == lane`. For the cross-thread case, partner thread = ((lane*VPT + pos) ^ j) / VPT, partner pos = ((lane*VPT + pos) ^ j) % VPT. We need both threads to know they're partners. Each thread processes its own values. For each value, it computes partner. If partner thread > current thread? We process each pair once by checking if logical index i < l. If i < l, thread i's side does the compare-swap and writes to both? But writing to partner requires shuffles back. Actually, for cross-thread compare-swap, simplest: each thread fetches its partner's value and index (2 shuffles). Then it knows both values. It computes what its own value should be after compare-swap. Since both threads do this symmetrically, each writes its own result. Total 2 shuffles per cross-thread compare-swap. But we need to ensure each pair is processed exactly once per sub-stage. Each logical index participates in exactly one pair per sub-stage. So each thread processes its VPT values, each with a partner. Some partners local, some remote. No pair processed twice because each logical index is owned by one thread. Good. Implementation: ```cuda template <int N, int VPT> __device__ void warp_bitonic_sort_desc(float val[VPT], int idx[VPT]) { #pragma unroll for (int k = 2; k <= N; k <<= 1) { #pragma unroll for (int j = k >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) { #pragma unroll for (int pos = 0; pos < VPT; ++pos) { int i = lane * VPT + pos; // lane = threadIdx.x & 31 int l = i ^ j; if (l > i) { // process pair once float other_val; int other_idx; int partner_thread = l / VPT; int partner_pos = l % VPT; if (partner_thread == lane) { other_val = val[partner_pos]; other_idx = idx[partner_pos]; } else { other_val = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val[partner_pos], partner_thread); other_idx = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, idx[partner_pos], partner_thread); } bool desc = ((i & k) == 0); bool swap; if (desc) swap = val[pos] < other_val; else swap = val[pos] > other_val; if (swap) { val[pos] = other_val; idx[pos] = other_idx; } } } } } } ``` Wait, but if `l < i`, we skip. But each logical index still needs to be processed. Since each pair has one index with `i < l` and one with `i > l`, when `i > l` we skip. But the thread owning `i` (where i > l) won't process that value in this sub-stage! That's wrong. In bitonic sort, each element must participate in exactly one compare-swap per sub-stage. The pair is (i, l) with l = i^j. Both elements must update. But if we only process when i < l, the element at i (where i < l) updates based on partner at l. The element at l doesn't update. But wait, when the thread owning l processes its value, it will have i=l, l=i^j=i (since XOR is symmetric). Then i > l, so it skips. So neither thread updates the element at l. Bug. We need to process the pair once but update both elements. The standard way is to process each element and determine its "target" based on whether it's the "low" or "high" side of the pair. For pair (a, b) with a < b (logical indices), the compare-swap result is: - If descending: position a gets max(a,b), position b gets min(a,b). - If ascending: position a gets min(a,b), position b gets max(a,b). Each thread, given its logical index i and partner l = i^j, can determine whether it's the lower or higher index. Let `is_low = i < l`. Then: - If descending: low gets max, high gets min. - If ascending: low gets min, high gets max. So each thread fetches partner value, then based on `is_low` and direction, decides whether to keep its value or partner's value. This updates both elements correctly without processing pair twice. Revised: ```cuda template <int N, int VPT> __device__ void warp_bitonic_sort_desc(float val[VPT], int idx[VPT]) { int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; #pragma unroll for (int k = 2; k <= N; k <<= 1) { #pragma unroll for (int j = k >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) { #pragma unroll for (int pos = 0; pos < VPT; ++pos) { int i = lane * VPT + pos; int l = i ^ j; float other_val; int other_idx; int partner_thread = l / VPT; int partner_pos = l % VPT; if (partner_thread == lane) { other_val = val[partner_pos]; other_idx = idx[partner_pos]; } else { other_val = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val[partner_pos], partner_thread); other_idx = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, idx[partner_pos], partner_thread); } bool is_low = i < l; bool desc = ((i & k) == 0); bool take_other; if (desc) take_other = (is_low ? val[pos] < other_val : val[pos] > other_val); else take_other = (is_low ? val[pos] > other_val : val[pos] < other_val); if (take_other) { val[pos] = other_val; idx[pos] = other_idx; } } } } } ``` This should be correct. Each thread processes its own values, fetches partner, decides keep or swap. For local partner (same thread), we can optimize by directly reading without shuffle. But for correctness, even reading local via shuffle would work (shuffle from same thread is fine). For performance, use local read. import torch
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

src = r'''
#include <cuda_runtime.h>
#include <float.h>
#include <torch/extension.h>

template <int N, int VPT>
__device__ void warp_bitonic_sort_desc(float val[VPT], int idx[VPT]) {
    int lane = threadIdx.x & 31;
    #pragma unroll
    for (int k = 2; k <= N; k <<= 1) {
        #pragma unroll
        for (int j = k >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) {
            #pragma unroll
            for (int pos = 0; pos < VPT; ++pos) {
                int i = lane * VPT + pos;
                int l = i ^ j;
                float other_val;
                int other_idx;
                int partner_thread = l / VPT;
                int partner_pos = l % VPT;
                if (partner_thread == lane) {
                    other_val = val[partner_pos];
                    other_idx = idx[partner_pos];
                } else {
                    other_val = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val[partner_pos], partner_thread);
                    other_idx = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, idx[partner_pos], partner_thread);
                }
                bool is_low = i < l;
                bool desc = ((i & k) == 0);
                bool take_other;
                if (desc) {
                    take_other = is_low ? (val[pos] < other_val) : (val[pos] > other_val);
                } else {
                    take_other = is_low ? (val[pos] > other_val) : (val[pos] < other_val);
                }
                if (take_other) {
                    val[pos] = other_val;
                    idx[pos] = other_idx;
                }
            }
        }
    }
}

// Test: each warp sorts 64 values (2 per thread)
__global__ void test64(const float* in, float* out) {
    int lane = threadIdx.x & 31;
    int warp = threadIdx.x >> 5;
    int base = warp * 64;
    float v[2];
    int idx[2];
    v[0] = in[base + lane * 2];
    v[1] = in[base + lane * 2 + 1];
    idx[0] = base + lane * 2;
    idx[1] = base + lane * 2 + 1;
    warp_bitonic_sort_desc<64, 2>(v, idx);
    out[base + lane * 2] = v[0];
    out[base + lane * 2 + 1] = v[1];
}

torch::Tensor run_test64(torch::Tensor x) {
    auto out = torch::empty_like(x);
    test64<<<1, 32>>>(x.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>());
    return out;
}
'''
cpp = r'''
#include <torch/extension.h>
torch::Tensor run_test64(torch::Tensor x);
'''
mod = load_inline(name='test_warp_sort', cpp_sources=cpp, cuda_sources=src,
                  functions=['run_test64'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'], verbose=False)

x = torch.randn(64).cuda()
out = mod.run_test64(x)
ref, _ = torch.sort(x, descending=True)
print('match 64:', torch.allclose(out, ref, atol=1e-5, rtol=1e-5))
print('max diff:', (out - ref).abs().max().item())

reasoning (2958 chars)
Warp sort for 64 is wrong. Let me debug. Maybe the direction logic or partner computation is off. Actually, I think the issue is that `__shfl_sync` reads from `partner_thread`, but the value at `partner_pos` in that thread might have been updated earlier in the same sub-stage by a previous `pos` iteration within the same thread. Since we process pos=0 then pos=1 within the same thread without synchronization, when pos=1 needs partner's value at pos=... the partner thread might be processing pos=0 or pos=1 at the same time. This is a race within a sub-stage! Within a sub-stage, all compare-swaps should happen simultaneously using values from the END of the previous sub-stage. But my code processes pos sequentially and reads partner's current value, which might have been updated in this sub-stage already. To fix, we need to read all partner values at the beginning of the sub-stage (before any updates), or use temporary arrays. Simpler: at start of sub-stage, each thread creates a copy of its VPT values. Then for each pos, read partner from the copy (not updated). Then write updates to the live array. Or write to a new array and swap at end. Actually, since all shuffles within a sub-stage should use values from previous sub-stage, we can make a copy `old_val = val` at the start of each sub-stage. Then all reads (local and remote) use `old_val`. Updates go to `val`. But for remote reads via shuffle, the partner thread also reads from its `old_val` (if all threads make copies at start). Good. So: ```cuda template <int N, int VPT> __device__ void warp_bitonic_sort_desc(float val[VPT], int idx[VPT]) { int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; float old_val[VPT]; int old_idx[VPT]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 2; k <= N; k <<= 1) { #pragma unroll for (int j = k >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) { // snapshot #pragma unroll for (int p = 0; p < VPT; ++p) { old_val[p] = val[p]; old_idx[p] = idx[p]; } __syncwarp(); // ensure all threads have snapshot? Actually shfl reads old_val which is local, so no need? #pragma unroll for (int pos = 0; pos < VPT; ++pos) { ... if (partner_thread == lane) { other_val = old_val[partner_pos]; other_idx = old_idx[partner_pos]; } else { other_val = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, old_val[partner_pos], partner_thread); other_idx = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, old_idx[partner_pos], partner_thread); } ... if (take_other) { val[pos] = other_val; idx[pos] = other_idx; } } } } } ``` Since all threads read from `old_val` which is their own snapshot, and shuffle reads partner's `old_val`, we avoid the race. `__syncwarp()` ensures all snapshots done before shuffles, though shuffles are warp-synchronous anyway. Let me test.

import torch
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

src = r'''
#include <cuda_runtime.h>
#include <float.h>
#include <torch/extension.h>

template <int N, int VPT>
__device__ void warp_bitonic_sort_desc(float val[VPT], int idx[VPT]) {
    int lane = threadIdx.x & 31;
    float old_val[VPT];
    int old_idx[VPT];
    #pragma unroll
    for (int k = 2; k <= N; k <<= 1) {
        #pragma unroll
        for (int j = k >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) {
            #pragma unroll
            for (int p = 0; p < VPT; ++p) {
                old_val[p] = val[p];
                old_idx[p] = idx[p];
            }
            __syncwarp();
            #pragma unroll
            for (int pos = 0; pos < VPT; ++pos) {
                int i = lane * VPT + pos;
                int l = i ^ j;
                float other_val;
                int other_idx;
                int partner_thread = l / VPT;
                int partner_pos = l % VPT;
                if (partner_thread == lane) {
                    other_val = old_val[partner_pos];
                    other_idx = old_idx[partner_pos];
                } else {
                    other_val = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, old_val[partner_pos], partner_thread);
                    other_idx = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, old_idx[partner_pos], partner_thread);
                }
                bool is_low = i < l;
                bool desc = ((i & k) == 0);
                bool take_other;
                if (desc) {
                    take_other = is_low ? (old_val[pos] < other_val) : (old_val[pos] > other_val);
                } else {
                    take_other = is_low ? (old_val[pos] > other_val) : (old_val[pos] < other_val);
                }
                if (take_other) {
                    val[pos] = other_val;
                    idx[pos] = other_idx;
                }
            }
        }
    }
}

__global__ void test64(const float* in, float* out) {
    int lane = threadIdx.x & 31;
    int warp = threadIdx.x >> 5;
    int base = warp * 64;
    float v[2];
    int idx[2];
    v[0] = in[base + lane * 2];
    v[1] = in[base + lane * 2 + 1];
    idx[0] = base + lane * 2;
    idx[1] = base + lane * 2 + 1;
    warp_bitonic_sort_desc<64, 2>(v, idx);
    out[base + lane * 2] = v[0];
    out[base + lane * 2 + 1] = v[1];
}

torch::Tensor run_test64(torch::Tensor x) {
    auto out = torch::empty_like(x);
    test64<<<1, 32>>>(x.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>());
    return out;
}
'''

"""Numeric distribution stress cases for correctness checks.

These cases are not hidden shapes. They rerun the same shape/seed validation
under a few scale regimes that catch kernels that only work for the nominal
N(0, 1)-ish inputs.
"""
from __future__ import annotations

import os
from collections.abc import Iterator, Mapping, Sequence
from contextlib import contextmanager
from dataclasses import dataclass, field

import torch

ToleranceOverride = Mapping[str, float | Mapping[str, float]]


@dataclass(frozen=True)
class NumericStressCase:
    name: str
    input_scales: Mapping[int, float] = field(default_factory=dict)
    state_scales: Mapping[str, float] = field(default_factory=dict)
    tolerance: ToleranceOverride | None = None


NOMINAL_CASE = NumericStressCase("nominal")

_SMALL_BF16 = {"bfloat16": {"atol": 1e-4, "rtol": 5e-2}}
_MED_BF16 = {"bfloat16": {"atol": 5e-4, "rtol": 5e-2}}
_LARGE_BF16 = {"bfloat16": {"atol": 2e-1, "rtol": 5e-2}}
_TINY_FP32 = {"float32": {"atol": 1e-7, "rtol": 1e-4}}
_LARGE_FP32 = {"float32": {"atol": 1e-1, "rtol": 1e-4}}

    "01_fp8_gemm": (
        NumericStressCase("small_input", input_scales={0: 1e-3}, tolerance=_SMALL_BF16),

        NumericStressCase(

            state_scales={"weight": 1e-2},
            tolerance=_SMALL_BF16,
        ),

def _scale_inputs(inputs: Sequence[object], scales: Mapping[int, float]) -> list[object]:
    out = list(inputs)
    for idx, scale in scales.items():
        if idx >= len(out):
            raise IndexError(f"input scale index {idx} out of range for {len(out)} inputs")
        value = out[idx]
        if not isinstance(value, torch.Tensor):
            raise TypeError(f"input {idx} is not a tensor and cannot be scaled")
        out[idx] = _scale_tensor(value, scale)
    return out


def _scale_states(
    models: Sequence[torch.nn.Module],
    scales: Mapping[str, float],
) -> list[tuple[torch.Tensor, torch.Tensor]]:
    backups: list[tuple[torch.Tensor, torch.Tensor]] = []
    if not scales:
        return backups
    for model in models:
        state = dict(model.named_parameters())
        state.update(dict(model.named_buffers()))
        for name, scale in scales.items():
            if name not in state:
                raise KeyError(f"state scale target {name!r} not found")
            tensor = state[name]
            if not torch.is_floating_point(tensor):
                raise TypeError(f"state scale target {name!r} is not floating point")
            backups.append((tensor, tensor.detach().clone()))
            with torch.no_grad():
                tensor.copy_(_scale_tensor(tensor, scale))
    return backups


def _scale_tensor(tensor: torch.Tensor, scale: float) -> torch.Tensor:
    if not torch.is_floating_point(tensor):
        raise TypeError(f"cannot scale n

120	// General k > 1 : warp-local bitonic merge + shared-memory final sort
121	// ----------------------------------------------------------------------------
122	
123	// Sort N elements distributed as VPT per thread (consecutive layout) within a
124	// single warp.  Logical index of vals[pos] is lane * VPT + pos.
125	template <int N, int VPT>
126	__device__ __forceinline__ void warp_bitonic_sort_desc(float val[VPT],
127	                                                        int   idx[VPT]) {
128	    int lane = threadIdx.x & 31;
129	    float old_val[VPT];
130	    int   old_idx[VPT];
131	
132	    #pragma unroll
133	    for (int k = 2; k <= N; k <<= 1) {
134	        #pragma unroll
135	        for (int j = k >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) {
136	            #pragma unroll
137	            for (int p = 0; p < VPT; ++p) {
138	                old_val[p] = val[p];
139	                old_idx[p] = idx[p];
140	            }
141	            __syncwarp();
142	
143	            #pragma unroll
144	            for (int pos = 0; pos < VPT; ++pos) {
145	                int i = lane * VPT + pos;
146	                int l = i ^ j;
147	                float other_val;
148	                int other_idx;
149	                int partner_thread = l / VPT;
150	                int partner_pos = l % VPT;
151	                if (partner_thread == lane) {
152	                    other_val = old_val[partner_pos];

+// Common setup / finalization for a row scan.  The per-warp merge step differs
+// between V_PER_THREAD == 1 (k <= 32) and V_PER_THREAD == 2 (k == 64), so we
+// expose it as a device function the two kernels can override inline.
+
+template <int K, int HEAP_PER_WARP>
+__device__ __forceinline__ void topk_row_finalize(float* s_val, int* s_idx,
+                                                   float* row_out_v, int64_t* row_out_i,
+                                                   int tid) {
+    constexpr int THREADS_PER_BLOCK = 1024;
+    bitonic_sort_desc<HEAP_PER_WARP * 32>(s_val, s_idx);
+    for (int i = tid; i < K; i += THREADS_PER_BLOCK) {
+        row_out_v[i] = s_val[i];
+        row_out_i[i] = (int64_t)s_idx[i];
+    }
+}

+template <int K, int HEAP_PER_WARP>
+__global__ void topk_kernel_v2(const float* __restrict__ x,

+    int64_t* row_out_i = out_idx + (int64_t)row * K;
+
+    constexpr int WARP_SIZE = 32;
+    constexpr int WARPS_PER_BLOCK = 32;
+    constexpr int THREADS_PER_BLOCK = WARP_SIZE * WARPS_PER_BLOCK;
+
+    int tid = threadIdx.x;
+    int lane = tid & (WARP_SIZE - 1);
+    int wid = tid >> 5;
+
+    int elements_per_warp = (N + WARPS_PER_BLOCK - 1) / WARPS_PER_BLOCK;
+    int warp_begin = wid * elements_per_warp;
+    int warp_end = min(warp_begin + elements_per_warp, N);
+
+    float vals[2];
+    int   idxs[2];
+    vals[0] = vals[1] = -FLT_MAX;
+    idxs[0] = idxs[1] = 0;
+
+    for (int base = warp_begin; base < warp_end; base += WARP_SIZE) {
+        int i = base + lane;
+        float new_val = (i < warp_end) ? row_x[i] : -FLT_MAX;
+        int new_idx = i;
+        float mval[4] = {vals[0], vals[1], new_val, -FLT_MAX};
+        int   midx[4] = {idxs[0], idxs[1], new_idx, 0};
+        warp_bitonic_sort_desc<128, 4>(mval, midx);
+        vals[0] = mval[0];
+        vals[1] = mval[1];
+        idxs[0] = midx[0];
+        idxs[1] = midx[1];
+    }
+
+    extern __shared__ char smem[];
+    float* s_val = (float*)smem;
+    int*   s_idx = (int*)(s_val + THREADS_PER_BLOCK * 2);
+

import torch
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

src = r'''
#include <cuda_runtime.h>
#include <float.h>
#include <torch/extension.h>

template <int N, int VPT>
__device__ void warp_bitonic_sort_desc(float val[VPT], int idx[VPT]) {
    int lane = threadIdx.x & 31;
    float old_val[VPT];
    int old_idx[VPT];
    #pragma unroll
    for (int k = 2; k <= N; k <<= 1) {
        #pragma unroll
        for (int j = k >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) {
            #pragma unroll
            for (int p = 0; p < VPT; ++p) {
                old_val[p] = val[p];
                old_idx[p] = idx[p];
            }
            __syncwarp();
            #pragma unroll
            for (int pos = 0; pos < VPT; ++pos) {
                int i = lane * VPT + pos;
                int l = i ^ j;
                float other_val;
                int other_idx;
                int partner_thread = l / VPT;
                int partner_pos = l % VPT;
                if (partner_thread == lane) {
                    other_val = old_val[partner_pos];
                    other_idx = old_idx[partner_pos];
                } else {
                    other_val = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, old_val[partner_pos], partner_thread);
                    other_idx = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, old_idx[partner_pos], partner_thread);
                }
                bool is_low = i < l;
                bool desc = ((i & k) == 0);
                bool take_other;
                if (desc) {
                    take_other = is_low ? (old_val[pos] < other_val) : (old_val[pos] > other_val);
                } else {
                    take_other = is_low ? (old_val[pos] > other_val) : (old_val[pos] < other_val);
                }
                if (take_other) {
                    val[pos] = other_val;
                    idx[pos] = other_idx;
                }
            }
        }
    }
}

__global__ void test128(const float* in, float* out) {
    int lane = threadIdx.x & 31;
    float v[4];
    int idx[4];
    for (int p = 0; p < 4; ++p) {
        v[p] = in[lane * 4 + p];
        idx[p] = lane * 4 + p;
    }
    warp_bitonic_sort_desc<128, 4>(v, idx);
    for (int p = 0; p < 4; ++p) {
        out[lane * 4 + p] = v[p];
    }
}

torch::Tensor run_test128(torch::Tensor x) {
    auto out = torch::empty_like(x);
    test128<<<1, 32>>>(x.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>());
    return out;
}
'''
cpp = r'''
#include <torch/extension.h>
torch::Tensor run_test128(torch::Tensor x);
'''
mod = load_inline(name='test_warp128', cpp_sources=cpp, cuda_sources=src,
                  functions=['run_test128'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'], verbose=False)

x = torch.randn(128).cuda()
out = mod.run_test128(x)
ref, _ = torch.sort(x, descending=True)
print('match 128:', torch.allclose(out, ref, atol=1e-5, rtol=1e-5))
print('max diff:', (out - ref).abs().max().item())
print('out first 10:', out[:10])
print('ref first 10:', ref[:10])

import torch
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

src = r'''
#include <cuda_runtime.h>
#include <torch/extension.h>

template <int N>
__device__ void bitonic_sort_desc(float* val, int* idx) {
    #pragma unroll
    for (int k = 2; k <= N; k <<= 1) {
        #pragma unroll
        for (int j = k >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) {
            int tid = threadIdx.x;
            for (int i = tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x) {
                int lo = i;
                int hi = i ^ j;
                if (hi > lo) {
                    if ((lo & k) == 0) {
                        if (val[lo] < val[hi]) {
                            float tv = val[lo]; val[lo] = val[hi]; val[hi] = tv;
                            int ti = idx[lo]; idx[lo] = idx[hi]; idx[hi] = ti;
                        }
                    } else {
                        if (val[lo] > val[hi]) {
                            float tv = val[lo]; val[lo] = val[hi]; val[hi] = tv;
                            int ti = idx[lo]; idx[lo] = idx[hi]; idx[hi] = ti;
                        }
                    }
                }
            }
            __syncthreads();
        }
    }
}

__global__ void test(const float* in, float* out) {
    extern __shared__ char smem[];
    float* s = (float*)smem;
    int* idx = (int*)(s + 1024);
    for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < 1024; i += blockDim.x) {
        s[i] = in[i];
        idx[i] = i;
    }
    __syncthreads();
    bitonic_sort_desc<1024>(s, idx);
    for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < 1024; i += blockDim.x) {
        out[i] = s[i];
    }
}

torch::Tensor run_test(torch::Tensor x) {
    auto out = torch::empty_like(x);
    test<<<1, 512, 1024*(sizeof(float)+sizeof(int))>>>(x.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>());
    return out;
}
'''
cpp = r'''
#include <torch/extension.h>
torch::Tensor run_test(torch::Tensor x);
'''
mod = load_inline(name='test_block512', cpp_sources=cpp, cuda_sources=src,
                  functions=['run_test'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'], verbose=False)

x = torch.randn(1024).cuda()
out = mod.run_test(x)
ref, _ = torch.sort(x, descending=True)
print('match 1024 block512:', torch.allclose(out, ref, atol=1e-5, rtol=1e-5))
print('max diff:', (out - ref).abs().max().item())

Block sort is also correct. So both components are correct individually. The issue must be in how they interact, or in the per-warp merge logic. Let me think: in `topk_kernel_v2`, each warp processes its chunk. For each batch, it loads 1 new value per thread, merges with 2 kept values via 128-element warp sort, keeps top 2. But wait, the warp sort of 128 elements includes the padding -FLT_MAX. After sort, the top 2 are the largest 2 of {kept0, kept1, new_val, -FLT_MAX}. If kept0 and kept1 are the top 2 of previous data, and new_val is from current batch, then after merge, the top 2 are the top 2 of (previous top 2) ∪ {new_val}. This is NOT the top 2 of all values seen so far! Because the previous top 2 might not include the third-largest value from previous batches. If new_val is between kept0 and kept1, we keep kept0 and new_val, discarding kept1. But kept1 might be needed if future new values are smaller than kept1. Actually, maintaining only top-2 per thread (total 64 per warp) is not enough if we process one element at a time. We need to keep the top-64 of all values seen so far, and when a new value arrives, merge it with the top-64 to get new top-64. My current approach keeps only top-2 per thread (64 per warp), and merges one new value at a time. But merging one new value with top-2 per thread only considers the thread's top-2, not the full top-64. Wait, the warp sort of 128 elements: each thread has 4 values: 2 kept + new + -inf. The warp collectively has 128 values (4*32). But the 2 kept values per thread are NOT the full top-64 of previous data. They're just the thread's local top-2 after previous merges. The full top-64 is distributed across the warp, with each thread holding 2 of them. When we sort 128 values (kept + new), we get the top 64 of (thread's kept 2) ∪ (all threads' new values)? No, that's not right either. Wait, actually, if each thread's 2 kept values are the top 2 values assigned to that thread, and all kept values across the warp form the top 64 of previous data, then sorting all kept + all new gives top 64 of (previous top 64) ∪ (new 32 values). That's correct! But are the thread's 2 kept values the top 2 of previous data assigned to that thread? No. After each merge, each thread keeps the top 2 of {its previous kept 0, kept 1, its new value, -inf}. This means the kept values are the top values from the thread's own new values, not globally across the warp. This is the bug! The warp-level merge should sort the entire warp's kept values + new values, then redistribute top 64 back to threads. But my approach only merges locally per thread. For example, thread 0 might have kept {10, 9}, thread 1 might have kept {8, 7}. New values: thread 0 gets 6, thread 1 gets 9.5. After local merge, thread 0 keeps {10, 9} (discards 6), thread 1 keeps {9.5, 8} (discards 7). But globally, top 64 should include 9.5, 10, 9, 8, 7... Actually 7 is still kept by thread 1. But what if thread 0 had kept {10, 5} and gets new 6? Local merge keeps {10, 6}, discards 5. But thread 1 might have had {9, 8}. Globally top 4 should be {10, 9, 8, 6}. My approach gives thread 0 {10,6}, thread 1 {9,8}. OK. But what if thread 0 had {10, 5}, gets new 4. Keeps {10,5}. Thread 1 had {9, 8}. OK. What if thread 0 had {10, 5}, gets new 7. Keeps {10,7}. Thread 1 had {9, 8}. Top 4 = {10,9,8,7}. OK. Hmm, maybe local merge works because the kept values are always the top values assigned to each thread? Wait no, after first batch, each thread's kept values are the top 2 of its first few values? No, after first merge, each thread keeps top 2 of {its initial -inf, -inf, its new value, -inf} = {new_value, -inf}. So thread 0 keeps {v0, -inf}, thread 1 keeps {v1, -inf}, etc. The warp collectively has {v0, -inf, v1, -inf, ...}. The actual top 64 includes v0..v31 and -inf. OK. After second batch, each thread merges {v0, -inf, v2_new, -inf} and keeps top 2. Thread 0 might keep {v0, v2_new}. Thread 1 keeps {v1, v3_new}. The warp collectively has top 64 = {all v's}. But what if v2_new is larger than v1? Then global top should include v2_new, v0, v1, etc. Thread 0 keeps {v0, v2_new}, thread 1 keeps {v1, v3_new}. The union includes v0, v1, v2_new, v3_new. Good. But what if v2_new is very large, larger than v1, but thread 1's kept doesn't include it. Thread 0 includes it. So the union still has it. Good. What if v2_new is between v1 and v3_new? Thread 0 keeps {v0, v2_new}. Thread 1 keeps {v1, v3_new}. Union has v0, v1, v2_new, v3_new. Good. What if thread 0 has {v0, v2_new} and thread 1 has {v1, v3_new}, but v2_new < v4_new (thread 2's second value). The union still has all of them. Sorting union at end gives correct top 64. Let's think: each thread maintains the top 2 values it has ever seen (from its own loaded elements). The union of all thread top-2s across the warp contains at most 64 values. Does it contain the global top 64 of all values loaded by the warp? Consider global top value T. It was loaded by some thread t. Thread t keeps top 2 of its own values, so T is in thread t's kept values (unless thread t has seen 2 values larger than T, but T is the global max, so impossible). So T is in the union. Similarly for any value in global top 64: it was loaded by some thread. That thread keeps top 2 of its values. If the value is not in the thread's top 2, then the thread has 2 values larger than it. Those 2 might be globally larger or smaller. But even if the value is not kept, it's possible those 2 larger values are also in global top 64, so we still have 64 values. But what if a value is in global top 64 but its thread has 2 larger values that are NOT in global top 64? That's impossible: if the thread's 2 values are larger than the value, and the value is in global top 64, then those 2 values are also in global top 64. So they're kept by some threads. But they might be kept by the same thread (using both slots), leaving no room for the original value. Example: Thread 0 loads values {100, 99, 98, ...}. Thread 1 loads {97, 96, ...}. Global top 64 includes 100,99,98,97,96,... Thread 0 keeps top 2 = {100, 99}, discards 98. Thread 1 keeps {97, 96}. So 98 is lost! But 98 is in global top 64. So the local top-2 per thread is NOT sufficient. My approach is wrong. I need to maintain the warp-wide top-64, not per-thread top-2. When merging 32 new values with 64 kept values, I need to sort all 96 values across the warp and keep top 64. This is what I intended with the warp bitonic sort of 128 elements (with padding), but I made each thread only sort its own 4 values, not the warp's 128 values. To sort the warp's 128 values, I need a warp-level bitonic sort where the 128 logical indices are distributed across the 32 threads. The `warp_bitonic_sort_desc<128,4>` I wrote does exactly that! It sorts 128 logical indices where thread t holds indices 4t, 4t+1, 4t+2, 4t+3. But in my kernel, I initialized the 4 values per thread as {vals[0], vals[1], new_val, -FLT_MAX}. This means logical index 4t = vals[0] (thread t's kept0), 4t+1 = vals[1] (thread t's kept1), 4t+2 = new_val (thread t's new), 4t+3 = -inf. But the kept values vals[0] and vals[1] are thread-local, not from logical indices 4t and 4t+1 globally. Wait, in the warp sort, logical index 4t is stored in thread t's val[0]. So thread 0's val[0] is logical index 0, thread 1's val[0] is logical index 4, etc. After sorting, thread t's val[0] is logical index 4t (the 4t-th largest of all 128 values). Thread t's val[1] is logical index 4t+1, etc. So after sort, if I keep val[0] and val[1] per thread, I keep logical indices 0,1,4,5,8,9,... which are NOT the top 64! I keep every other pair, missing indices 2,3,6,7,... That's the bug! After a global warp sort, the top 64 are logical indices 0..63. With VPT=4, these are distributed as: - Thread 0: indices 0,1,2,3 - Thread 1: indices 4,5,6,7 - ... - Thread 15: indices 60,61,62,63 So to keep top 64, each thread should keep ALL 4 values (for threads 0..15), and threads 16..31 should keep 0? But we only have 2 slots per thread. This means with VPT=4, after sorting 128, we can't keep top 64 by keeping 2 per thread. We need to either: 1. Keep VPT=4 per thread after sort (total 128 per warp), then sort final 2048 and keep top 64. But this doubles register usage. 2. Use a different distribution: keep 2 per thread but ensure the top 64 logical indices are exactly those 64 kept positions. For option 2, if we use interleaved layout instead of consecutive, where thread t holds logical indices t and t+32 (for kept), and new value at t+64 or t+96, then after sorting, top 64 might be distributed as thread t holding indices t and t+32. Let me think. Actually, with interleaved layout: thread t holds logical indices t, t+32, t+64, t+96. After sort descending, top 64 are indices 0..63. These are held by: - Thread 0: 0, 32 - Thread 1: 1, 33 - ... - Thread 31: 31, 63 So if each thread keeps 2 values (val[0] and val[1]), and val[0] corresponds to logical index t, val[1] to logical index t+32, then after sort, thread t keeps top values at indices t and t+32, which are exactly in top 64. So I should use interleaved layout! Let me redesign the warp bitonic sort to use interleaved layout where thread t holds logical indices t, t+32, t+64, ... (i.e., VPT values spaced by 32). For VPT values per thread, logical index of `val[pos]` is `lane + pos * 32`. For compare-swap of logical index i and partner l=i^j: - Thread holding i: thread = i % 32, pos = i / 32. - Thread holding l: thread = l % 32, pos = l / 32. For j < 32, partner l = i^j has same high bits, so pos is same, thread differs. For j >= 32, partner l = i^j differs in high bits, so pos differs, thread may be same or different. Example N=128, VPT=4, interleaved: - Thread t holds indices t, t+32, t+64, t+96. - For j=1: i=t, l=t^1. pos=0 for both. Different threads. Compare-swap top two values? - For j=32: i=t, l=t+32. Same thread, pos 0 and 1. Local compare. - For j=64: i=t, l=t+64. Same thread, pos 0 and 2. Local. This layout makes cross-thread shuffles more frequent for small j, but local for large j. Actually, for a descending final sort, we want top values at low indices (0,1,2,...). With interleaved layout, after sort, thread t holds indices t (top 32) and t+32 (next 32). Keeping val[0] and val[1] means keeping top 64. For N=64, VPT=2, interleaved: thread t holds t and t+32. After sort, keeping val[0] and val[1] keeps top 64. Good. So I need to change the warp bitonic sort to interleaved layout. For k=64, in the merge: - vals[0] and vals[1] are the kept top-64 values, stored interleaved: vals[0] is logical index lane (top 32), vals[1] is logical index lane+32 (next 32). - new_val should be placed at logical index lane+64 or lane+96? 5	the heap via a single warp-min reduction + ballot.  After the scan, warps
6	write their local top-K into shared memory, a bitonic sort produces the global
7	top-K, and the first K entries are written out.
